##                our previous 0..=7 stub accepted it.
##              * Reads sched_priority from the user-provided
##                param pointer (NULL -> EFAULT, unreadable ->
##                whatever linux_errno_for maps the
##                page-fault to).
##              * Validates priority/policy compatibility via
##                a new sched_priority_check_for_policy
##                helper: RT policies (FIFO=1, RR=2) require
##                prio in [1,99]; OTHER/BATCH/IDLE/DEADLINE/
##                EXT require prio == 0; anything else
##                returns EINVAL.
##              * For RT policies, enforces RLIMIT_RTPRIO via
##                rlimit_rtprio_check_for_caller /
##                rlimit_rtprio_check_with.  Default
##                rlim_cur(RTPRIO) = 0 means no priority in
##                [1,99] passes, so a fresh process cannot
##                grant itself real-time scheduling — exactly
##                the privilege the rlimit is supposed to
##                gate.  Violation returns EPERM, matching
##                Linux's __sched_setscheduler.
##         - sys_sched_setparam:
##              * Validates the user param pointer and reads
##                sched_priority.
##              * Since our modelled current policy is always
##                SCHED_OTHER, any non-zero sched_priority
##                returns EINVAL.
##         - New named index pcb::RLIMIT_RTPRIO_INDEX = 14.
##         - New helpers (mirroring the FSIZE/NICE pair shape):
##              read_user_sched_priority(ptr) -> Result<i32, i32>
##              sched_priority_check_for_policy(policy, prio)
##              rlimit_rtprio_check_for_caller(prio)
##              rlimit_rtprio_check_with(soft, prio)
##      Coverage:
##         - dispatch_linux: sched_setscheduler with policy 8,
##           4 -> EINVAL; with valid policy + NULL param ->
##           EFAULT; sched_setparam(0, NULL) -> EFAULT.
##         - sched_priority_check_for_policy decision table:
##              (OTHER, 0) -> Ok; (OTHER, 1) -> EINVAL
##              (FIFO, 1)  -> Ok; (FIFO, 99) -> Ok
##              (FIFO, 0)  -> EINVAL; (RR, 100) -> EINVAL
##              (BATCH, 0) -> Ok; (BATCH, 1) -> EINVAL
##              (99, 0)    -> EINVAL (unknown policy)
##         - rlimit_rtprio_check_with decision table:
##              (INF, 99)   -> Ok (always allow under INFINITY)
##              (0,   1)    -> EPERM (default-deny)
##              (50, 50)    -> Ok (== ceiling)
##              (50, 51)    -> EPERM (above ceiling)
##              (99, 99)    -> Ok (full RT range)
##         - rlimit_rtprio_check_for_caller in kernel context
##           always Ok (bypass rule).
##      Choice rationale:
##         * The Linux contract for sched_setscheduler is
##           that the kernel returns specifically EPERM (not
##           EACCES) for RLIMIT_RTPRIO violations because the
##           rlimit-derived denial is treated as a privilege
##           check.  We mirror that errno exactly so programs
##           that distinguish EACCES vs EPERM (some service
##           managers do) behave the same way as on Linux.
##         * Param pointer validation is now required even
##           for SCHED_OTHER.  Previously NULL was silently
##           accepted, which was wrong; Linux always reads
##           sched_priority from the struct and the field
##           must be 0 for non-RT policies.  Programs that
##           passed NULL "because OTHER doesn't need a
##           priority" were relying on undocumented
##           behavior; the new EFAULT matches what they'd
##           see on Linux.
##         * The kernel-context bypass for the rlimit gate
##           is what lets the existing scheduler self-tests
##           (run during boot before any real userspace
##           process exists) continue to work without
##           special-casing.
##      Limitation: sched_setattr is still a stub.
##      sched_setattr is the modern replacement for
##      sched_setscheduler that takes a sched_attr struct
##      (with separate fields for OTHER, FIFO/RR, and
##      DEADLINE).  Wiring up DEADLINE parameter validation
##      (runtime / deadline / period) is a separate batch.
##      Limitation: sched_setscheduler still doesn't
##      actually switch the target task to the requested
##      policy — our scheduler is single-class round-robin
##      and the Linux RT policies aren't honored beyond
##      ABI-shape validation.  Setting SCHED_FIFO with a
##      valid priority returns 0 but the task's actual
##      scheduling behavior is unchanged.

##   76. fsync / fdatasync / syncfs fd validation (batch 50).
##      Status: DONE for ABI shape.  The four sync-family
##      syscalls (fsync=74, fdatasync=75, sync=162, syncfs=
##      306) previously returned 0 unconditionally — they
##      didn't even check whether the fd was open.  That
##      meant programs that relied on fsync to report errors
##      (databases, journaling layers, file rename-and-sync
##      patterns) would silently lose the EBADF signal that
##      should have told them their fd table was corrupt.
##      Linux contract that we now enforce:
##         - fsync(fd) / fdatasync(fd) / syncfs(fd):
##              EBADF if fd is not a valid file descriptor
##              in the caller's table.
##         - fsync(fd) / fdatasync(fd):
##              EINVAL if fd refers to a Pipe or Console
##              (the Linux equivalent of "Operation not
##              supported for this fd type" — Linux returns
##              EINVAL for pipes/sockets/character devices
##              that don't have a backing block device).
##         - syncfs(fd):
##              0 for any valid fd kind.  Linux's syncfs
##              doesn't require a regular file: it looks
##              up the superblock containing the fd's inode
##              and flushes that.  Console/Pipe fds in our
##              model don't have a backing FS but we accept
##              them best-effort, matching the spirit of
##              the Linux semantics (the caller asked us
##              to flush "the filesystem containing this
##              fd" — if there isn't one, we have nothing
##              to flush, so trivially 0).
##         - sync(): 0 unconditionally (no fd arg, no
##              parameters to validate).
##      Implementation:
##         - sys_fsync routes via lookup_caller_fd, then
##           matches on HandleKind to gate
##           File→0 vs Console|Pipe→EINVAL.
##         - sys_fdatasync delegates to sys_fsync (our FS
##           has no metadata write-back layer to skip).
##         - sys_syncfs only checks lookup_caller_fd; any
##           valid fd yields 0.
##         - sys_sync is unchanged (always 0).
##      Boot self-test coverage:
##         - sync() → 0 (no-fd path).
##         - fsync/fdatasync/syncfs with fd=0 from kernel
##           context (no caller_pid → no fd table) → EBADF.
##         - fsync/fdatasync/syncfs with fd=-1 → EBADF.
##         - Real-fd-kind branches (File→0, Console|Pipe→
##           EINVAL for fsync) are not covered by the boot
##           self-test because the self-test runs without a
##           Linux fd table; they are exercised indirectly
##           (any regression that re-introduces the
##           unconditional-zero behavior would fail the
##           EBADF assertions above).
##      Limitation: durability is still a stub.  None of
##      these syscalls actually flush a buffer cache to
##      durable storage because we don't have a unified
##      buffer cache, and our current FS is in-memory only.
##      Programs that depend on fsync for crash-consistency
##      (SQLite, PostgreSQL, leveldb-style journal layers,
##      file-rename-and-sync atomic-write patterns) get the
##      Linux error semantics correctly but get NO actual
##      durability guarantee.  This will land when:
##         - a real storage driver layer exists (ext4 or
##           similar with a backing block device); and
##         - the VFS has a write-back cache that knows how
##           to map fd → owning superblock → dirty page
##           writeback queue.
##      Until then, fsync-as-durability-fence is a no-op.
##      Documented here so future audits don't mistake the
##      now-passing EBADF tests for "fsync works".

##   77. Per-process umask storage (batch 51).
##      Status: PARTIAL.  Round-trip is now correct; VFS
##      consumption is not yet wired.
##      Before batch 51, sys_umask was a hard-coded stub:
##      every call returned 0o022 and the argument was
##      silently discarded.  That broke the "save and
##      restore" idiom programs use during sandbox setup:
##         int old = umask(0o077);
##         ... do privileged work ...
##         umask(old);
##      With the old stub, `old` always came back as
##      0o022 regardless of what was actually in effect,
##      so the second umask call would install 0o022
##      instead of whatever the caller had inherited.
##      Some test suites (notably postfix's, sendmail's,
##      and parts of glibc's locale install scripts)
##      gate on `umask(0) == umask()` and would fail.
##      Wiring up:
##         - Added pcb::Process::linux_umask: u16
##           (default 0o022, masked to 0o777 on write).
##         - Added pcb::get_umask(pid) -> Option<u16>.
##         - Added pcb::set_umask(pid, new) -> Option<u16>
##           returning the old mask, masking new with
##           & 0o777 (Linux silently drops higher bits).
##         - fork_create copies parent's linux_umask
##           verbatim (POSIX inheritance).
##         - sys_umask now routes through these helpers
##           when caller_pid is Some.  Kernel-context
##           callers (boot self-test) fall back to the
##           0o022 default so the dispatch path stays
##           usable before any process exists.
##      Boot self-test coverage:
##         - Kernel-context dispatch: umask(0), umask(0o777),
##           umask(u64::MAX) all return 0o022.
##         - Per-process direct test on a dummy PID:
##              fresh PID → 0o022 default
##              set 0o077 → returns Some(0o022),
##                          get returns Some(0o077)
##              set 0o022 → returns Some(0o077),
##                          get returns Some(0o022)
##              set 0o7777 → returns Some(0o022),
##                           get returns Some(0o777)
##                           (high bits silently dropped)
##              destroy + set on destroyed PID → None
##              destroy + get on destroyed PID → None
##      Limitation: the VFS does NOT yet consult
##      linux_umask at file creation time.  A program that
##      does `umask(0o077); creat(file, 0o666);` will
##      still get the kernel's default permissions on the
##      created inode rather than `0o666 & ~0o077 = 0o600`
##      as Linux would.  What works now is the round-trip
##      property (what most callers actually depend on);
##      the actual permission-masking on creat/openat/mkdir
##      is a separate wiring task.  When that lands, the
##      VFS open path should call pcb::get_umask(caller_pid)
##      and mask the requested mode before storing it.

##   78. setuid / setgid wired to credentials (batch 52).
##      Status: PARTIAL.  setuid/setgid are real; the
##      multi-arg variants are still silent stubs.
##      Before batch 52, sys_setuid and sys_setgid were
##      silent-success stubs: they accepted any argument
##      and returned 0 without updating the PCB.  That meant
##      programs that drop privileges with
##           setuid(real_uid);
##           if (getuid() != real_uid) abort();
##      would always abort, because getuid() reads
##      credentials.uid (which was unchanged) while the
##      caller thought setuid had applied.  This is a
##      genuine correctness bug — programs that gate
##      privileged operations on a successful setuid would
##      run with the wrong identity invisibly.
##      Wiring up:
##         - sys_setuid now reads pcb::get_credentials,
##           validates the arg, updates credentials.uid,
##           writes back via pcb::set_credentials.
##         - sys_setgid mirrors setuid but updates
##           credentials.gid.  The permission check still
##           keys on credentials.uid (only root may change
##           gid arbitrarily), matching Linux's CAP_SETGID
##           gating in the absence of a real cap subsystem.
##      Linux semantics that we enforce:
##         - (uid_t)-1 / (gid_t)-1 (u32::MAX): EINVAL.
##           Linux reserves INVALID_UID for "no change" in
##           setresuid; passing it to setuid is rejected.
##         - Caller uid == 0: any target uid accepted.
##         - Caller uid != 0 with target != current: EPERM.
##         - Caller uid != 0 with target == current: 0
##           (no-op success — the idiom that
##           privilege-dropping startup code relies on).
##         - Kernel context (no caller_pid): no-op success,
##           since the kernel has no Linux credentials.
##      Boot self-test coverage:
##         - Dispatch path: setuid(0) / setgid(0) → 0 in
##           kernel context.
##         - Dispatch path: setuid((uid_t)-1) /
##           setgid((gid_t)-1) → EINVAL even without a
##           PCB.
##         - Per-process round-trip on a dummy PID:
##              fresh creds (0, 0) read back correctly
##              uid := 1000 via set_credentials
##              get returns (1000, 0)  — gid unchanged
##              self-set uid := 1000 again is a no-op.
##      Limitations (each tracked as a future batch):
##         - setreuid/setregid/setresuid/setresgid are
##           still silent-success stubs.  Linux's "any of
##           the three args may be (uid_t)-1 = no change"
##           semantics needs careful translation into our
##           single-uid model, and the per-arg permission
##           check (CAP_SETUID gating) needs the same
##           caller-uid-keyed check as setuid.  Until then,
##           a program that calls setresuid(1000, 1000,
##           1000) instead of setuid(1000) still gets the
##           silent-success-with-wrong-uid bug.
##         - setfsuid/setfsgid still silently return 0
##           instead of the previous fsuid/fsgid.  Linux's
##           "fsuid" concept is uid-for-FS-access-checks,
##           separate from euid; we don't track it
##           separately and the VFS has no permission
##           checks anyway, so the only thing that breaks
##           is the return value (callers that use it to
##           save+restore fsuid).
##         - getgroups/setgroups still silently accept
##           everything.  Real implementation needs the
##           Vec<u32> supp-groups field on
##           ProcessCredentials wired through, plus EFAULT
##           on bad user pointers.
##         - We carry only one uid (no separate ruid /
##           euid / suid).  Programs that read all three
##           via getresuid see the same value for all
##           three, which is correct for a process that
##           hasn't done a partial transition.  Adding
##           separate fields is a follow-up.

##   79. setreuid / setregid / setresuid / setresgid wired
##      to credentials (batch 53).
##      Status: PARTIAL.  The selection rule + EPERM gating
##      is real; the single-uid model means partial
##      transitions are folded.
##      Before batch 53, all four were silent-success
##      stubs.  After batch 52 fixed sys_setuid the
##      multi-arg variants were the obvious remaining hole
##      — programs that use setresuid(N, N, N) or
##      setreuid(N, N) instead of plain setuid(N) (musl's
##      pthread_setname_np helpers, some daemons) still
##      hit the silent-success-with-wrong-uid bug.
##      Wiring up:
##         - apply_uid_change(ruid, euid, suid) helper
##           selects the resolved new uid by priority:
##           euid wins over ruid wins over suid.  u32::MAX
##           is Linux's (uid_t)-1 "no change" sentinel and
##           is skipped during selection.  All -1 = no-op
##           success.  When a non-(-1) value resolves, the
##           helper applies the same permission gate as
##           sys_setuid (caller uid 0 may do anything; non-
##           root may only set to its current uid).
##         - apply_gid_change mirrors with gid; the
##           permission gate still keys on uid (CAP_SETGID
##           in Linux's model).
##         - sys_setreuid / sys_setresuid call
##           apply_uid_change with (ruid, euid, -1) and
##           (ruid, euid, suid) respectively.  Same for the
##           gid variants.
##      Why "euid wins":
##         - In our single-uid model the PCB uid serves as
##           both real and effective.  Programs that call
##           setresuid(-1, X, -1) mean "I want my effective
##           uid to be X" — the dominant pattern in sudo-
##           style privilege transitions.  Picking euid
##           first matches that intent.
##         - For setresuid(X, -1, -1) (real-uid-only
##           change, far less common), our model still
##           updates uid to X.  getuid() then returns X,
##           which is what a program checking the *real*
##           uid expects.  geteuid() (aliased to getuid)
##           also returns X — that part diverges from
##           Linux, but a caller that asks for euid to
##           stay alone while changing ruid is already
##           assuming we track them separately, which we
##           don't and document.
##      Boot self-test coverage:
##         - Pure-function apply_uid_change /
##           apply_gid_change with (-1,-1,-1) → 0.
##         - Pure-function with each of (X,-1,-1),
##           (-1,X,-1), (-1,-1,X) in kernel context → 0
##           (no PCB, no-op).
##         - Dispatch routing for SETREUID / SETREGID /
##           SETRESUID / SETRESGID with all-(-1) → 0.
##      Limitations:
##         - We carry one uid, not three.  A program that
##           does setresuid(0, 1000, 0) (drop euid only,
##           keep ability to regain root via saved uid)
##           cannot be faithfully represented; our model
##           reads euid first so we'd set uid=1000 and
##           the program loses its saved-uid handle.  Real
##           Linux semantics for this pattern require
##           three separate fields.  Tracked as a future
##           credentials-subsystem batch.
##         - We never actually deny based on RLIMIT_NPROC
##           after a uid change (NPROC is checked at fork
##           against the new uid by the next fork call;
##           that wiring is already in place via
##           batch 43).  No new accounting needed here.

##   80. setfsuid / setfsgid + getgroups / setgroups wired
##      to credentials (batch 54).
##      Status: setfsuid/setfsgid: return-value correct.
##              getgroups/setgroups: real, with the Linux
##              error surface and credentials.groups
##              backing the data.
##      Before batch 54, setfsuid/setfsgid always returned
##      0 regardless of the calling process's actual
##      fs-uid/fs-gid.  Programs that use the round-trip
##      idiom
##           old = setfsuid(N);
##           ... privileged FS op ...
##           setfsuid(old);
##      would restore the wrong fsuid (the literal 0,
##      regardless of the real "previous" value).  Linux's
##      setfsuid never errors (the return *is* the
##      previous fsuid), so callers don't have a defensive
##      check — they just use the returned value.
##      Wiring up setfsuid/setfsgid:
##         - Read credentials.uid (resp. gid) and return
##           it as the "previous" fsuid.  We don't track
##           fsuid separately from uid, so in our model
##           fsuid == uid and the round-trip is trivially
##           correct.
##         - Don't mutate state — we have no VFS
##           permission checks that would observe a
##           changed fsuid anyway.
##         - Kernel context (no caller_pid): return 0
##           (no PCB to read).
##      Wiring up getgroups:
##         - size < 0 -> EINVAL.
##         - size == 0 -> return ngroups (the count), no
##           write.
##         - size > 0 && size < ngroups -> EINVAL.
##         - size > 0 && ngroups == 0 -> return 0 (no
##           pointer touch).
##         - size > 0 && ngroups > 0 && list == NULL
##           -> EFAULT.
##         - Else: copy_to_user the ngroups u32s, return
##           ngroups.
##         - Kernel context: 0 groups.
##      Wiring up setgroups:
##         - size > NGROUPS_MAX (65536) -> EINVAL.
##         - caller uid != 0 -> EPERM (approximates
##           CAP_SETGID).
##         - size == 0 -> drop all supp groups, return 0.
##         - size > 0 && list == NULL -> EFAULT.
##         - Else: copy_from_user n u32s, install into
##           credentials.groups, return 0.
##         - Kernel context: silent success without
##           touching the PCB.
##      Boot self-test coverage:
##         - setfsuid/setfsgid kernel-ctx returns 0.
##         - setfsuid/setfsgid round-trip via direct PCB
##           manipulation on a dummy PID (verifies the
##           return-value path reads credentials.uid).
##         - getgroups(size=-1) -> EINVAL.
##         - getgroups(size=0) kernel-ctx -> 0.
##         - getgroups(size=16) kernel-ctx -> 0.
##         - setgroups(size=65537) -> EINVAL.
##         - setgroups(0, NULL) kernel-ctx -> 0.
##         - setgroups drop-all via direct PCB set
##           (verifies clearing the groups vec sticks).
##      Limitations:
##         - We still don't track fsuid separately, so
##           the *behavioural* part of setfsuid (changing
##           which uid the VFS uses for permission
##           checks) is unimplemented.  But the VFS has
##           no permission checks anyway — no observable
##           divergence.  When the VFS grows real ACL
##           enforcement, add a separate fsuid field to
##           credentials and route setfsuid through it.
##         - setgroups EPERM is approximated by "uid !=
##           0" instead of CAP_SETGID.  Equivalent in
##           practice given we don't have setuid-bit
##           binaries.
##         - getgroups always reports the on-PCB supp
##           groups; we never auto-include the gid in
##           the supp list (Linux doesn't either — that's
##           the caller's responsibility).
##         - copy_from_user error in setgroups is folded
##           into linux_errno_for, which currently maps
##           InvalidAddress -> EFAULT.  No special
##           handling for partial reads.
##
##   81. personality() wired to per-process storage
##      (batch 55).
##      Status: dispatch contract correct.  Storage real
##              but flags are not yet acted on.
##
##      Before batch 55, sys_personality was a hard-coded
##      "return 0" stub that ignored its argument entirely.
##      A program that did
##           old = personality(ADDR_NO_RANDOMIZE);
##           personality(old);
##      would see ADDR_NO_RANDOMIZE silently dropped and
##      its subsequent query return 0 — which gdb in
##      particular treats as a configuration error.
##
##      What was done:
##        - Added pub linux_personality: u32 to pcb::Process
##          with default 0 (PER_LINUX).  Inherited verbatim
##          across fork_create (Linux propagates personality
##          across fork; only execve resets it).
##        - Added pcb::get_personality / pcb::set_personality
##          helpers (parallel to get_umask / set_umask) for
##          callers outside the syscall path.
##        - Rewrote sys_personality:
##           * arg0 == 0xffffffff is the canonical "query
##             only" sentinel — return current personality
##             unchanged.  Glibc startup and gdb both poke
##             this to learn the starting personality.
##           * arg0 with a non-zero low byte (any persona
##             other than PER_LINUX) is rejected with
##             EINVAL.  This matches Linux's behaviour when
##             the personality is one it doesn't know.
##           * arg0 with persona byte 0 and arbitrary flag
##             bits is accepted, the value stored on the
##             PCB verbatim, and the previous value
##             returned.
##           * Kernel context (caller_pid() == None) falls
##             back to a query that returns 0 and a set
##             that no-ops but still returns 0 — matching
##             what userspace would see if started from
##             init with no parent persona override.
##
##      Self-test coverage:
##        - personality(0xffffffff) kernel-ctx -> 0.
##        - personality(0x10000) kernel-ctx -> 0 (the
##          flag-bits-only set path that succeeds).
##        - personality(0x00000001) -> EINVAL (non-PER_LINUX
##          persona byte rejected).
##        - PCB round-trip on a dummy PID: fresh PCB reads
##          back 0; set(0x40000) returns 0; subsequent get
##          returns 0x40000; set(0) returns 0x40000.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        - The flag bits are stored but the kernel does
##          not act on any of them.  ADDR_NO_RANDOMIZE is
##          a no-op because we don't randomize address
##          space.  READ_IMPLIES_EXEC is a no-op because
##          our mmap flags don't grant exec implicitly for
##          read mappings.  MMAP_PAGE_ZERO is a no-op
##          because we don't map page 0 at all.  When
##          those subsystems grow ASLR / mmap-flag
##          handling, route the relevant decisions through
##          pcb::get_personality.
##        - execve does not currently reset personality to
##          0.  Linux resets persona at execve unless
##          PER_LINUX2 is set; we'll need to add a
##          set_personality(pid, 0) call to the ELF loader
##          path when we get exec wired up properly.
##          Track separately when execve grows real
##          semantics.
##
##   82. PR_SET_NAME / PR_GET_NAME backed by pcb.name
##      (batch 56).
##      Status: real round-trip; UTF-8 limitation
##              documented.
##
##      Before batch 56, sys_prctl handled PR_SET_NAME
##      (option 15) as "silent success that ignores the
##      argument" and PR_GET_NAME (option 16) as "write 16
##      zero bytes to the user buffer (or skip if NULL)".
##      A program that did
##           prctl(PR_SET_NAME, "worker");
##           prctl(PR_GET_NAME, buf);
##      would always see an empty comm, breaking strace
##      output, systemd's "MainPID=" notification matching,
##      and gdb's `info thread` (which all rely on
##      per-thread comm).
##
##      What was done:
##        - Added pcb::set_name(pid, new) -> Option<old>
##          (parallel to pcb::name() lookup that already
##          existed).  Stores the new String verbatim;
##          truncation and NUL handling live at the
##          syscall call-site so the helper stays generic.
##        - Rewrote PR_SET_NAME:
##           * Reads 16 bytes from user pointer via
##             copy_from_user (validates the range,
##             STAC/CLAC-guarded).
##           * Truncates at first NUL or 15 bytes
##             (Linux's TASK_COMM_LEN-1 = 15 visible chars,
##             16th byte reserved for terminator).
##           * Validates UTF-8 (see limitation below);
##             non-UTF-8 -> EINVAL.
##           * Calls pcb::set_name on caller_pid().
##             Kernel context (no PCB) silently succeeds.
##           * NULL user buffer: Linux behaviour is to
##             ignore (no change, returns 0); we match.
##        - Rewrote PR_GET_NAME:
##           * NULL user buffer -> EFAULT (Linux returns
##             EFAULT because copy_to_user fails on NULL;
##             previously we returned 0 here, which was
##             wrong).
##           * Validates 16-byte writable user range.
##           * Reads pcb::name(caller_pid) into a 16-byte
##             zero-initialised buffer (16th byte stays
##             NUL).  Truncates at 15 visible chars.
##           * Kernel context: returns 16 zero bytes
##             (matches what an unnamed task would see).
##
##      Self-test coverage:
##        - prctl(PR_SET_NAME, NULL) -> 0 (silent ignore).
##        - prctl(PR_GET_NAME, NULL) -> -EFAULT
##          (replaces the old "returns 0" stub assertion).
##        - PCB round-trip on a dummy PID: create("orig")
##          reads back "orig"; set_name("new") returns
##          "orig"; subsequent name() returns "new".
##        - 15-byte truncation invariant: storing a
##          15-char string (the pre-truncated form of a
##          16-char input that PR_SET_NAME would chop)
##          reads back unchanged.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        - pcb.name is a Rust String, so we reject
##          non-UTF-8 PR_SET_NAME inputs with EINVAL.
##          Linux accepts arbitrary bytes (comm is a
##          byte buffer, not a UTF-8 string).  Every
##          userspace program we have seen sets ASCII
##          comms, so this is observationally equivalent
##          for real workloads — but it is a deviation.
##          When the rest of the codebase migrates from
##          String to Vec<u8> for OS-boundary names per
##          CLAUDE.md, switch pcb.name to Vec<u8> and
##          drop the UTF-8 validation here.
##        - Per-thread comm: Linux gives each thread its
##          own comm; we currently share the per-process
##          PCB name across all threads.  When threads
##          grow their own PCB-equivalent storage, route
##          PR_SET_NAME/PR_GET_NAME to per-thread state
##          instead.
##        - PR_SET_NAME from kernel context is a no-op
##          (we have no PCB to write).  The caller still
##          gets a success return, which is the right
##          observation since kernel-context callers
##          never check the comm afterwards.
##
##   83. uname/sethostname/setdomainname wired to
##      nameservice + EPERM gate (batch 57).
##      Status: real round-trip.  CAP_SYS_ADMIN
##              approximated as uid==0.
##
##      Before batch 57, sethostname/setdomainname were
##      silent-accept stubs and uname always returned
##      nodename="localhost", domainname="localdomain"
##      regardless of any sethostname call.  Programs that
##      did `sethostname("foo"); read /proc/sys/kernel/
##      hostname` (or the uname equivalent) would always
##      see localhost — breaking hostnamectl, init
##      scripts, and anything that reads the nodename to
##      log its identity.
##
##      What was done:
##        - sethostname: copy_from_user into a 64-byte
##          stack buffer; validate UTF-8 (deviation —
##          documented limitation); gate CAP_SYS_ADMIN
##          equivalent on uid==0 (kernel ctx bypasses);
##          push through fs::nameservice::set_hostname.
##          len==0 -> EINVAL (nameservice rejects empty).
##        - setdomainname: same shape but len==0 is a
##          legal "clear domain" operation (Linux
##          accepts).
##        - uname: read nodename and domainname from the
##          nameservice get_hostname/get_domain getters,
##          falling back to "localhost"/"localdomain" if
##          nameservice isn't initialised yet.
##        - All three eagerly call nameservice::
##          init_defaults() (idempotent) so the syscall
##          works even if no kshell command has woken
##          nameservice up yet.
##
##      Self-test coverage:
##        - sethostname(NULL,0) -> -EINVAL (replaces the
##          old "returns 0" assertion).
##        - setdomainname(NULL,0) -> 0 (clear-domain).
##        - sethostname(NULL,5) -> -EFAULT.
##        - sethostname(len=65) -> -EINVAL.
##        - Round-trip: set_hostname("ouros-test") +
##          get_hostname() reads back; same for domain.
##          Originals restored at end of test.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        - Non-UTF-8 input rejected with EINVAL.  Linux
##          accepts any bytes.  Tie-break: nameservice
##          stores Rust String.  Migrate when nameservice
##          state migrates to Vec<u8>.
##        - CAP_SYS_ADMIN is approximated by uid==0
##          instead of being a real per-task capability
##          set.  Same caveat as setgroups in batch 54.
##        - Kernel context bypasses the uid==0 gate.
##          That matches the bootstrap usage (we want
##          init to be able to set the hostname) but
##          would need revisiting if any kernel module
##          that doesn't have full privilege starts
##          calling sethostname.
##
##   84. sysinfo procs field wired to pcb::count
##      (batch 58).
##      Status: real live-process count.  Thread
##              under-reporting documented.
##
##      Before batch 58, sys_sysinfo left the `procs`
##      field (offset 80..82 in struct sysinfo) at zero
##      while filling in uptime/totalram/freeram.  Tools
##      that surface "number of processes" via sysinfo
##      (htop's fallback path, /proc/loadavg approximations,
##      various monitoring shims) would see 0 — implying
##      a dead system — even with hundreds of live PCBs.
##
##      What was done:
##        - sys_sysinfo now writes pcb::count() as a u16
##          at offset 80..82, saturating at u16::MAX as
##          Linux does (the field is `unsigned short`).
##        - Self-test verifies the underlying count helper
##          increments on pcb::create and decrements on
##          pcb::destroy, and that the saturating cast at
##          u16::MAX clamps as expected.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        - We model one PCB per process; Linux counts
##          every kernel-visible thread (LWP) in this
##          field, so workloads with many threads per
##          process will under-report.  When threads
##          grow their own kernel-visible structure,
##          switch this field to "total LWP count" and
##          have sys_clone with CLONE_THREAD bump a
##          separate counter.
##        - Saturation at u16::MAX is silent.  Linux
##          truncates the same way and userspace
##          accommodates it.  No errno needed.
##
##   85. BUG: sched_fairness::self_test() is flaky.
##      Status: observed crash; root cause unknown.
##
##      During batch 58 boot testing, the
##      [sched_fairness] Running self-test... line was
##      followed by a KERNEL PANIC with the message
##      truncated mid-line.  Re-running boot-test.sh
##      immediately produced a PASS with no code
##      changes.  The same flakiness was visible at the
##      start of the session before batch 55 was
##      committed (initial boot test failed; subsequent
##      ones passed).
##
##      Reproduction is intermittent.  Likely root
##      causes:
##        - Race in sched::all_task_ticks() between the
##          measurement loop and the scheduler updating
##          tick counters concurrently.
##        - One of the assertions in fairness self-test
##          (JFI bounds check, equal-distribution check)
##          fires when the per-task tick deltas land in
##          a degenerate distribution (e.g. a single
##          short-running task gets all the ticks in the
##          window).
##        - Serial output buffering ate the panic
##          message so we don't know which assertion
##          fired.
##
##      Next steps when revisiting:
##        - Read assert/panic info from QEMU's debug
##          exit code (debugcon.log) instead of relying
##          on serial echo.
##        - Add a try-catch wrapper around the self-test
##          that prints a "[sched_fairness] FAILED:
##          <assertion message>" line before re-raising,
##          so the test failure is forensically visible.
##        - If the JFI assertion is the issue, widen
##          the tolerance window from ~500..520 to
##          ~480..540 or similar.
##
##   86. set_robust_list / get_robust_list backed by
##      per-task storage (batch 59).
##      Status: registration + read-back are real.
##              On-exit robust-mutex walk is NOT
##              implemented and is documented below.
##
##      Before batch 59:
##        - sys_set_robust_list ignored the head pointer
##          entirely and returned 0 (head was never
##          recorded), so any later get_robust_list
##          would not have been able to honour glibc's
##          ABI-compat check even in principle.
##        - sys_get_robust_list validated its user
##          pointers and then returned ENOSYS, which
##          glibc treats as "no robust list" — fine for
##          a workload that doesn't use robust mutexes,
##          but it precludes ever shipping them.
##
##      What was done:
##        - thread_clone now owns a ROBUST_LIST map keyed
##          by TaskId, with register_robust_list /
##          lookup_robust_list helpers symmetric with the
##          existing CLEAR_CHILD_TID map.
##        - sys_set_robust_list rejects len != 24 with
##          EINVAL (sizeof(struct robust_list_head) on
##          x86_64) and otherwise stores (head, len) for
##          the calling task.  head == 0 unregisters.
##        - sys_get_robust_list now: writes head (or NULL
##          if unregistered) to *head_ptr and len (or 24)
##          to *len_ptr, then returns 0; refuses cross-
##          task inspection with EPERM (no ptrace
##          capability).  pid == 0 and pid == gettid()
##          both resolve to the current task.
##        - on_thread_exit_hook drops the robust-list
##          entry unconditionally so the table does not
##          grow across thread lifetimes.
##        - Self-tests exercise: bad pid -> EINVAL,
##          NULL head_ptr -> EFAULT, set_robust_list
##          bad len -> EINVAL, valid set+lookup round
##          trip via the storage layer, NULL re-register
##          clears the entry, cross-task get -> EPERM.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        - We do NOT walk the robust list on thread
##          exit and wake its futexes.  That is the
##          PTHREAD_MUTEX_ROBUST_NP cleanup protocol; a
##          program that opts into robust mutexes today
##          will see those mutexes stay locked when an
##          owning thread dies abruptly.  Fix requires
##          (a) a user-pointer-readable context at the
##          point of on_thread_exit_hook and (b) the
##          actual list walk + futex_wake loop, neither
##          of which is hard but both of which need
##          their own self-test for the walk's bounded-
##          iteration guard.  We have no current
##          consumer of robust mutexes, so it stays
##          here as a documented gap.
##        - EPERM for cross-task get_robust_list is
##          strictly stricter than Linux (Linux allows
##          it with PTRACE_MODE_READ_REALCREDS or
##          CAP_SYS_PTRACE).  Until we have a ptrace
##          cap and a way for trusted debuggers to
##          present it, refusing is the safe answer —
##          but if a system monitor turns up that needs
##          to inspect other tasks, this gate is the
##          place to relax.
##        - ESRCH-vs-EPERM ordering: Linux distinguishes
##          "task does not exist" (ESRCH) from "task
##          exists but we can't see it" (EPERM).  We
##          collapse both into EPERM today.  A future
##          refinement could check pcb / task tables and
##          return ESRCH for truly-missing TIDs, which
##          would let strace surface a more accurate
##          message.
##
##   87. getrandom flag validation tightened to match
##      Linux EINVAL behaviour (batch 60).
##      Status: known flags only; mutually-exclusive
##              source bits rejected.
##
##      Before batch 60, sys_getrandom blindly ignored
##      its `flags` argument: any bit pattern was
##      accepted, including future Linux flags we do not
##      know about and the explicitly-illegal
##      GRND_RANDOM | GRND_INSECURE combination.  Letting
##      unknown bits silently slip through meant any
##      future Linux extension would appear to "work" on
##      our kernel while doing none of the requested
##      behaviour — a silent ABI drift trap.
##
##      What was done:
##        - sys_getrandom now rejects flags that contain
##          any bit outside (GRND_NONBLOCK | GRND_RANDOM
##          | GRND_INSECURE) with EINVAL.
##        - GRND_RANDOM | GRND_INSECURE together is also
##          rejected with EINVAL (matches Linux >= 5.6:
##          the two flags request mutually-exclusive
##          entropy semantics).
##        - Self-tests exercise: unknown bit (0x10) ->
##          EINVAL, RANDOM|INSECURE -> EINVAL, buflen 0
##          with NONBLOCK -> 0, valid 8-byte read with
##          no flags -> 8, valid reads with each of the
##          three single-source flags -> 4.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        - We still do not distinguish behaviour between
##          GRND_RANDOM, GRND_NONBLOCK, and GRND_INSECURE
##          at runtime — they all draw from the same
##          ChaCha20 CSPRNG.  This is consistent with how
##          modern Linux behaves once entropy is
##          initialised (urandom == random == insecure
##          for byte stream quality), so we are not
##          misleading callers.

## 88. Linux ABI batch 61: real per-PCB
##     PR_SET_PDEATHSIG / PR_GET_PDEATHSIG storage.
##
##     Background: prctl options 1 (PR_SET_PDEATHSIG) and 2
##     (PR_GET_PDEATHSIG) were previously stubbed —
##     PR_SET_PDEATHSIG silently accepted any signal value
##     and discarded it; PR_GET_PDEATHSIG was unhandled and
##     fell through to ENOSYS via the catch-all.  This made
##     well-behaved Linux daemons (which often arm
##     PDEATHSIG to clean up worker processes on supervisor
##     exit) believe the kernel had no opinion on whether
##     their request was accepted, and prevented them from
##     querying current state to make policy decisions.
##
##     What was done:
##       - Added `linux_pdeathsig: u32` to the per-process
##         PCB (`Process` in kernel/src/proc/pcb.rs).
##         Default 0 ("disabled") in Process::new and
##         explicitly reset to 0 in fork_create, matching
##         Linux's invariant that PDEATHSIG does NOT
##         inherit across fork.
##       - Added pcb::get_pdeathsig / set_pdeathsig
##         helpers that take the PROCESS_TABLE lock and
##         return Option<u32>.
##       - sys_prctl option 1 (PR_SET_PDEATHSIG) now:
##           * Rejects sig > 64 with EINVAL (Linux's
##             accepted range is 0..=64 where 0 means
##             "disabled").
##           * Stores the value via pcb::set_pdeathsig
##             keyed on caller_pid().  Kernel-context
##             callers (caller_pid() == None) succeed
##             without storing — same as our existing
##             pattern for other per-PCB prctls.
##       - sys_prctl option 2 (PR_GET_PDEATHSIG) now:
##           * Rejects NULL user buffer with EFAULT.
##           * Reads the stored value, copies it as i32
##             to the user int* via copy_to_user (SMAP
##             safe with kernel-context bypass).
##           * Kernel-context defaults to 0 (disabled).
##       - Self-tests exercise:
##           * PR_SET_PDEATHSIG(65) -> EINVAL
##           * PR_SET_PDEATHSIG(0|15|64) -> 0
##           * PR_GET_PDEATHSIG(NULL) -> EFAULT
##           * PR_GET_PDEATHSIG(&scratch) -> 0 with scratch
##             populated as 0 (kernel-ctx default).
##           * pcb::set/get_pdeathsig storage round-trip:
##             create -> default 0, set 15 -> prior 0,
##             query -> 15, replace 9 -> prior 15, disable
##             0 -> prior 9, destroy -> None for both
##             helpers.
##
##     Known limitations (documented gaps, NOT bugs):
##       - We do not actually deliver the death signal on
##         parent exit.  That requires:
##           1. A signal-delivery path for user processes
##              (we have a stubbed signal subsystem but no
##              user-pending-signal queue + handler-frame
##              setup yet), and
##           2. A parent-exit lifecycle hook that scans
##              children, reads linux_pdeathsig, and posts
##              the signal.
##         Both are tracked under the larger "POSIX
##         signal support" gap (see roadmap signal entry).
##         Until that lands, PR_SET_PDEATHSIG is a faithful
##         store-and-query at the ABI level but a no-op at
##         the behavioural level.  This is consistent with
##         how strace appears to programs ("succeeded —
##         maybe the death just hasn't happened yet") and
##         is preferable to ENOSYS (which would make
##         daemons fall back to a less-correct cleanup
##         strategy).
##       - We should also reset linux_pdeathsig on execve
##         (Linux clears it across exec).  We currently
##         only reset on fork.  The exec path will need
##         the same single-line reset added when the
##         dedicated exec PCB-reset block is touched
##         next.

## 89. Linux ABI batch 62: real per-PCB sched_policy /
##     sched_priority storage with full round-trip
##     across sched_(get|set)scheduler / sched_(get|set)param.
##
##     Background: sched_setscheduler previously validated
##     a request (policy whitelist, priority range,
##     RLIMIT_RTPRIO gate) and then silently discarded the
##     result.  Subsequent sched_getscheduler always
##     reported SCHED_OTHER (0) and sched_getparam always
##     wrote priority 0 back to the user buffer.  Programs
##     that round-trip their scheduling parameters (a
##     common sanity check immediately after a setter
##     call, performed e.g. by glibc's nptl bootstrap and
##     by every RT-aware runtime such as PulseAudio,
##     JACK, the Real-Time Java VM and ROS) would see a
##     mismatch between "the value I just set" and "the
##     value the kernel reports back" and either log a
##     spurious error or fall into a retry loop.
##
##     What was done:
##       - Added `linux_sched_policy: u32` (default 0 =
##         SCHED_OTHER) and `linux_sched_priority: i32`
##         (default 0) to the Process struct in
##         kernel/src/proc/pcb.rs.
##       - Both fields are inherited verbatim across fork
##         in fork_create — matching Linux's default
##         behaviour (SCHED_RESET_ON_FORK is opt-in, see
##         limitation below).
##       - Added pcb::{get,set}_sched_policy and
##         pcb::{get,set}_sched_priority helpers.
##       - sys_sched_setscheduler now stores the validated
##         (policy, priority) tuple via the helpers after
##         the existing whitelist + range + RTPRIO
##         checks pass.  Kernel-context callers with no
##         caller_pid() skip the store (consistent with
##         every other per-PCB linux ABI write).
##       - sys_sched_getscheduler now resolves pid==0 to
##         the caller, reads the stored policy via
##         pcb::get_sched_policy, and returns it (default
##         0 if no PCB).
##       - sys_sched_getparam now reads the stored
##         priority via pcb::get_sched_priority and writes
##         it to the user's struct sched_param.
##       - sys_sched_setparam now looks up the target's
##         current policy, validates the requested
##         priority against it (so e.g. updating only the
##         priority of an existing SCHED_FIFO task works,
##         instead of always EINVAL'ing on non-zero), and
##         stores the new priority.  Also runs the RTPRIO
##         gate when the current policy is RT.
##       - Self-tests exercise:
##           * Storage helpers: defaults are (0, 0);
##             set/get round-trips for SCHED_FIFO@50,
##             SCHED_RR@99, back to SCHED_OTHER@0; after
##             destroy all four helpers return None.
##           * sys_sched_getscheduler on a freshly-stored
##             SCHED_FIFO PCB reports 1 (not 0).
##           * sys_sched_getparam writes the stored
##             priority (42) to a kernel-stack scratch
##             buffer.
##           * sys_sched_getscheduler on a bogus pid
##             returns ESRCH (unchanged).
##           * sys_sched_setparam on a SCHED_OTHER PCB
##             with prio 0 succeeds; with prio 5 returns
##             EINVAL (validates against the current
##             stored policy, not always against
##             SCHED_OTHER).
##
##     Known limitations:
##       - SCHED_RESET_ON_FORK (bit 0x40000000 OR'd into
##         the policy at sched_setscheduler time) is not
##         honoured.  Linux clears the RT policy/priority
##         in fork'd children of a task that has set this
##         flag, instead of inheriting.  Wiring it would
##         require detecting the bit in the setter,
##         masking it out before storing, and recording
##         it in a separate per-PCB boolean that
##         fork_create consults.  Documented as a small
##         follow-up.
##       - The stored policy/priority do NOT influence
##         actual scheduling decisions yet — our scheduler
##         is single priority-round-robin and ignores
##         SCHED_FIFO / SCHED_RR semantics entirely.  This
##         is the same situation as before this batch
##         (the ABI was always advisory at the behavioural
##         level); what changed is that the ABI now
##         round-trips honestly.  Real RT scheduling is
##         tracked under the scheduler roadmap and is the
##         right place for that work.

## 90. Linux ABI batch 63: real per-PCB nice value with
##     round-trip across getpriority(2) / setpriority(2).
##
##     Background: setpriority previously validated the
##     request (which whitelist, range clamp to [-20, 19],
##     RLIMIT_NICE gate when boosting) and then silently
##     discarded the result.  getpriority always reported
##     `20 - 0 = 20` regardless of what the caller had
##     set.  Programs that round-trip nice (e.g. shell
##     `renice $$` followed by `nice` to display, or
##     systemd's `Nice=` directive that re-reads after
##     setting) would observe nice=0 forever.
##
##     What was done:
##       - Added `linux_nice: i32` (default 0) to the
##         Process struct in kernel/src/proc/pcb.rs.
##         Inherited verbatim across fork (matches Linux:
##         nice is inherited and also preserved across
##         exec).
##       - Added pcb::get_nice / set_nice helpers.
##       - sys_setpriority now resolves the target pid —
##           * PRIO_PROCESS (which=0) + who!=0 -> named
##             process,
##           * everything else (which=PGRP/USER, or
##             which=0 with who=0) -> caller_pid()
##         — and stores the clamped nice value via
##         pcb::set_nice after the existing range and
##         RLIMIT_NICE checks.
##       - sys_getpriority resolves the same way, reads
##         the stored nice via pcb::get_nice (defaulting
##         to 0 if no PCB), and returns `20 - nice` (the
##         Linux ABI quirk that lets callers distinguish
##         negative-nice results from -errno).
##       - Self-tests exercise:
##           * Storage helpers: defaults to 0; set/get
##             round-trips for 10, 19, then -5; after
##             destroy both helpers return None.
##           * sys_getpriority(PRIO_PROCESS, real_pid)
##             after set_nice(19) reports 1 (= 20 - 19).
##           * sys_setpriority(real_pid, -5) succeeds and
##             pcb::get_nice reads back -5.
##           * sys_getpriority for the same pid reports
##             25 (= 20 - -5).
##           * Out-of-range +100 silently clamps to +19
##             and stores it.
##
##     Known limitations:
##       - The stored nice does NOT affect the actual
##         scheduler decisions yet.  Same situation as
##         batch 62 (sched_policy / sched_priority): the
##         ABI now round-trips honestly but our scheduler
##         is single priority-round-robin and treats
##         every task uniformly.  Real nice-aware
##         scheduling lives under the scheduler roadmap.
##       - PRIO_PGRP and PRIO_USER target *every* process
##         in the group / owned by the uid; we store/read
##         only against the caller's PCB for now (a
##         partial implementation that's correct for the
##         common case of "renice myself" or "look up my
##         own nice value", which is what every shell
##         command does).  A full implementation would
##         walk the PCB table by pgrp/uid; that walks the
##         same gap we already noted for setpgid /
##         getpgid (no real pgrp / session tracking yet).

## 91. Flaky ktrace wrap-around self-test (FIXED in
##     batch 63 as a drive-by).
##
##     Symptom: `assertion left == right failed: left:
##     602, right: 600` at kernel/src/ktrace.rs:501 during
##     boot.  Reproduces intermittently — observed once in
##     batch 63's first build, did not reproduce on
##     immediate re-run.
##
##     Cause: the wrap-around test recorded 600 General
##     events in a loop and asserted that
##     `total_events()` increased by exactly 600.  But
##     `total_events()` counts every category; if a timer
##     interrupt fires inside the 600-iteration window and
##     records a Sched CONTEXT_SWITCH event, the delta
##     becomes 601 or 602 and the assertion blows up.
##     Race window grew slightly as more dispatch self-
##     tests were added to linux.rs (batches 59 onward
##     shifted the timing such that the ktrace test now
##     runs late enough to catch a tick).
##
##     Fix: mask all categories except General around the
##     loop, then restore the saved mask.  Now only our
##     own General events can be recorded during the
##     window; timer/IRQ/Sched activity is mask-dropped.
##     Three immediate re-runs of the boot test PASSED
##     after the fix.


## 92. Linux ABI batch 64: validate ARCH_SET_FS address
##     against the x86_64 user-space ceiling.
##
##     Before this batch, `sys_arch_prctl(ARCH_SET_FS, addr)`
##     blindly forwarded `addr` to
##     `WRMSR(IA32_FS_BASE, addr)`.  Two real bugs:
##
##       (a) Non-canonical addresses (anything where bits
##           63..47 are not a sign-extension of bit 47, e.g.
##           u64::MAX, or any value in 0x0000_8000_0000_0000
##           ..0xffff_7fff_ffff_ffff) cause WRMSR to raise
##           #GP per Intel SDM Vol 3A §4.4.  Since we entered
##           via a syscall the #GP fires in kernel mode and
##           panics the kernel — a one-line userspace
##           denial-of-service.
##
##       (b) Canonical kernel addresses (the high half) were
##           accepted.  They can't be dereferenced from user
##           mode (would page-fault on first use), but
##           letting userspace install a kernel pointer into
##           FS is the kind of thing that turns a future
##           leak primitive into a privilege probe.  Linux
##           closes this with the same TASK_SIZE_MAX check;
##           we now mirror it.
##
##     Fix: introduce
##     `const USER_FS_BASE_MAX: u64 = 1u64 << 47;` and
##     `if addr >= USER_FS_BASE_MAX { return -EPERM; }`
##     before the `WRMSR`.  This matches Linux's behaviour
##     and is the value of TASK_SIZE_MAX on 4-level paging
##     vintage x86_64 (which is what we boot under).
##
##     Self-tests cover four cases via dispatch_linux:
##       * addr == 1 << 47          → -EPERM (boundary)
##       * addr == 0xffff_ffff_8000_0000 → -EPERM (kernel)
##       * addr == u64::MAX         → -EPERM (non-canonical)
##       * addr == (1 << 47) - 0x1000 → 0 (valid user)
##
##     Because the success case actually clobbers
##     IA32_FS_BASE — which the kernel can rely on for FS-
##     relative state — the test saves the MSR with RDMSR
##     before the sequence and restores it after each
##     branch.
##
##     Known follow-up (deferred): the CLONE_SETTLS path in
##     `kernel/src/proc/thread_clone.rs` (where a new thread
##     gets its FS base set from the clone3 args) does not
##     yet apply the same validation.  An attacker calling
##     clone3 with a non-canonical `tls` could crash the
##     kernel on the new thread's first dispatch.  Should be
##     a small follow-up batch — extract the validation into
##     a helper (`is_canonical_user_fs_base`) in syscall::
##     linux and reuse it from thread_clone.
##
##     RESOLVED in batch 65 (see entry 93 below).


## 93. Linux ABI batch 65: extend ARCH_SET_FS validation to
##     the CLONE_SETTLS path.
##
##     The follow-up flagged in entry 92.  Before this batch,
##     `linux_clone` passed `args.new_tls` (= `frame.arg4`)
##     through to `thread_clone::clone_thread` unvalidated.
##     The cloned thread's trampoline (`clone_thread_trampoline`
##     in `kernel/src/proc/thread_clone.rs`) then did
##     `WRMSR(IA32_FS_BASE, new_tls)` for any non-zero
##     `new_tls`.  Same #GP-on-non-canonical hazard as the
##     direct `arch_prctl(ARCH_SET_FS, …)` path, except the
##     crash fires on the NEW thread's first scheduler
##     dispatch — even more confusing to debug.  Reachable by
##     any userspace via `clone(CLONE_VM | CLONE_THREAD |
##     CLONE_SETTLS, …)` with a poisoned `tls` argument.
##
##     Fix: hoist `USER_FS_BASE_MAX = 1 << 47` to a module-
##     level `pub(crate) const` in `syscall/linux.rs` so both
##     call sites use the same canonical-user ceiling.  Then
##     in `linux_clone`, immediately after extracting
##     `new_tls`, gate on `CLONE_SETTLS` being set AND
##     `new_tls != 0` AND `new_tls >= USER_FS_BASE_MAX`, and
##     return `-EPERM` on hit.
##
##     Why the gate is at the dispatch site and not in
##     `clone_thread`: returning EPERM cleanly requires
##     bypassing the `KernelError -> errno` mapping (the
##     closest `KernelError::PermissionDenied` maps to EACCES,
##     not EPERM).  Keeping the policy at the Linux-ABI layer
##     makes the errno choice direct and matches Linux's own
##     `do_arch_prctl_64` which is what
##     `arch/x86/kernel/process.c:set_new_tls` reaches
##     through.
##
##     Why gate on CLONE_SETTLS: without that flag, `arg4` is
##     not interpreted as a TLS address — it could be any
##     garbage value the caller happened to leave in R8.  The
##     trampoline currently honours `new_tls != 0` regardless
##     of the flag, which is itself a latent bug (the args
##     struct should clear new_tls when CLONE_SETTLS is
##     unset).  That bug is now masked by the dispatch gate
##     for any input that would actually trigger #GP, but
##     belongs on the cleanup list:
##
##       TODO (small): in `linux_clone`, force
##       `args.new_tls = 0` when `CLONE_SETTLS` is not set, so
##       the trampoline's "0 = leave MSR alone" invariant
##       holds regardless of caller hygiene.  Not security-
##       critical given the dispatch gate, just hygiene.
##
##     Self-tests added to the existing clone-frame block:
##       * tls == 1 << 47           → -EPERM (boundary)
##       * tls == 0xffff_ffff_8000_0000 → -EPERM (kernel half)
##       * tls == u64::MAX          → -EPERM (non-canonical)
##       * tls == (1 << 47) - 0x1000 → -ESRCH (valid, reaches
##         clone_thread which then bails on no-owning-process)
##       * CLONE_SETTLS unset + garbage tls → -ESRCH (gate
##         must not fire)
##       * CLONE_SETTLS set + tls == 0 → -ESRCH (gate must
##         not fire on the "leave MSR alone" sentinel)
##
##     Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).


## 94. Linux ABI batch 66: real per-PCB PR_SET_DUMPABLE /
##     PR_GET_DUMPABLE round-trip with proper default.
##
##     Before this batch, prctl(PR_SET_DUMPABLE, …) silently
##     accepted anything and returned 0, and prctl(
##     PR_GET_DUMPABLE) always returned 0 (the
##     "SUID_DUMP_DISABLE" sentinel).  Two real problems:
##
##       (a) Programs that read PR_GET_DUMPABLE see 0 and
##           conclude the process opted out of core dumps —
##           Linux defaults to 1 (SUID_DUMP_USER).  systemd
##           reads this during service start to decide whether
##           to install a coredump handler; getting 0 back
##           where Linux returns 1 trips its "process is in
##           a weird state" path.
##
##       (b) The value didn't round-trip at all: PR_SET_DUMPABLE
##           followed by PR_GET_DUMPABLE always returned 0
##           even if the caller set 1.  Programs that use
##           this as a flag (e.g. setting it to 0 before
##           dropping privileges, checking it back later to
##           confirm) saw their write silently ignored.
##
##     Fix: add `linux_dumpable: u32` to the PCB (default 1 =
##     SUID_DUMP_USER, matching Linux), with `pcb::get_dumpable`
##     / `pcb::set_dumpable` helpers.  Inherited verbatim
##     across fork (Linux: fork preserves dumpable).
##     PR_SET_DUMPABLE now validates user range (0 or 1 only;
##     2 = SUID_DUMP_ROOT is privileged and Linux rejects it
##     from userspace) and persists via the helper.
##     PR_GET_DUMPABLE reads the helper and returns the
##     stored value; kernel context falls back to the
##     SUID_DUMP_USER default.
##
##     Known limitation (deferred): Linux RESETS dumpable to 1
##     on every successful execve (unless the binary is
##     setuid, in which case it becomes 2 transiently).  We
##     preserve across exec instead of resetting, because we
##     don't have a Linux-ABI exec-time hook for this yet.
##     Equivalent to PR_SET_DUMPABLE(0) being "sticky across
##     exec" — a userspace program that disables dumpability
##     and then execve's a child will see dumpable=0 in the
##     child where Linux would reset to 1.  None of the
##     userspace we test against currently relies on the
##     reset semantics; flag as a small follow-up when we add
##     the exec-time PCB cleanup hook (which will also need
##     to reset linux_personality among others, per the
##     existing `personality` doc comment).
##
##     Self-tests cover both layers:
##       * PR_SET_DUMPABLE rejects 2, 3, 1000, u64::MAX with
##         EINVAL (gate fires before the pcb store).
##       * PR_SET_DUMPABLE(0) and (1) both succeed.
##       * PR_GET_DUMPABLE returns the default (1) in kernel
##         context (no PCB).
##       * pcb::{get,set}_dumpable round-trips through
##         0 -> 1 -> 2 -> destroy -> None.
##
##     Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 19s).


## 95. Linux ABI batch 67: real per-PCB PR_SET_KEEPCAPS /
##     PR_GET_KEEPCAPS round-trip.
##
##     Same problem as entry 94 (dumpable), one notch over.
##     PR_SET_KEEPCAPS silently accepted anything and
##     PR_GET_KEEPCAPS always returned 0.  No round-trip:
##     setting 1 still read 0 back.  Programs that drop
##     privileges via setuid and check PR_GET_KEEPCAPS to
##     confirm whether their caps will survive saw the wrong
##     answer.
##
##     Fix: add `linux_keepcaps: u32` to the PCB (default 0 =
##     KEEPCAPS_CLEAR, matching Linux), with `pcb::get_keepcaps`
##     / `pcb::set_keepcaps` helpers.  Inherited verbatim
##     across fork.  PR_SET_KEEPCAPS validates user range (0
##     or 1; anything else is EINVAL) and persists.
##     PR_GET_KEEPCAPS reads the stored value.
##
##     Same exec-reset limitation as dumpable: Linux RESETS
##     keepcaps to 0 on every successful execve, we preserve.
##     Tracked under the same exec-hook follow-up.
##
##     The flag has no effect on actual privilege transitions
##     because we don't model POSIX capability sets; it
##     exists purely for ABI round-trip.  systemd and dbus
##     both probe it during startup.
##
##     Self-tests cover both layers:
##       * PR_SET_KEEPCAPS rejects 2, 3, 100, u64::MAX with
##         EINVAL.
##       * PR_SET_KEEPCAPS(0) and (1) both succeed.
##       * PR_GET_KEEPCAPS returns the default (0) in kernel
##         context.
##       * pcb::{get,set}_keepcaps round-trips 0 -> 1 -> 0
##         -> destroy -> None.
##
##     Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).


## 96. Linux ABI batch 68: real per-PCB PR_SET_NO_NEW_PRIVS
##     sticky-flag round-trip with strict Linux argument
##     validation, and corrected default for PR_GET.
##
##     Before this batch, PR_SET_NO_NEW_PRIVS silently
##     accepted anything and PR_GET_NO_NEW_PRIVS always
##     returned 1 (the "paranoid" answer chosen on the theory
##     that we don't honour setuid so NNP is "true by
##     construction").  Both wrong:
##
##       (a) Returning 1 always is a lie when userspace never
##           set the bit.  Linux's default is 0; sandbox-aware
##           code that reads PR_GET_NO_NEW_PRIVS to decide
##           "should I tighten the seatbelt before exec'ing
##           an untrusted child?" got the wrong answer.
##
##       (b) PR_SET_NO_NEW_PRIVS accepted any value with no
##           argument validation.  Linux enforces a strict
##           shape: arg2 == 1, arg3 == arg4 == arg5 == 0; any
##           deviation is EINVAL.  We accepted PR_SET_NO_NEW_PRIVS(0)
##           silently, which is a "clear NNP" call Linux never
##           supports (the bit is monotone-sticky).
##
##     Fix: add `linux_no_new_privs: u32` to the PCB (default
##     0, matching Linux), with `pcb::get_no_new_privs` /
##     `pcb::set_no_new_privs` helpers.  Inherited verbatim
##     across fork.  Preserved across exec by design (sticky
##     monotone semantics — sandbox parents rely on NNP
##     propagating through `fork`+`execve`).
##
##     PR_SET_NO_NEW_PRIVS now mirrors Linux's argument check:
##     arg2 must be 1, arg3/arg4/arg5 must all be 0, otherwise
##     EINVAL.  PR_GET_NO_NEW_PRIVS likewise rejects non-zero
##     arg2..arg5 with EINVAL.  Both read/write through the
##     PCB; kernel context falls back to the cleared default.
##
##     The sticky semantics ("once 1, never 0") fall out of
##     the surface — the only successful PR_SET path stores 1,
##     and there is no other ABI path that touches the flag.
##     The pcb helper itself is *not* sticky (it stores any
##     u32) so future exec-time code can manipulate it
##     legitimately.
##
##     Self-tests cover:
##       * PR_SET arg2 in {0, 2, u64::MAX} -> EINVAL.
##       * PR_SET arg2=1 with arg3/arg4/arg5 == 1 (each in
##         turn) -> EINVAL.
##       * PR_SET arg2=1 with all auxiliaries 0 -> 0.
##       * PR_GET with any non-zero auxiliary -> EINVAL.
##       * PR_GET in kernel context -> 0 (default).
##       * pcb storage layer round-trips 0 -> 1 -> 0 -> destroy
##         -> None.  (Existing PR_GET_NO_NEW_PRIVS test that
##         expected 1 was updated to expect 0 — flagged here
##         as a deliberate ABI-correctness fix, not a
##         regression.)
##
##     Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).


## 97. Linux ABI batch 69: real per-PCB PR_SET_CHILD_SUBREAPER
##     / PR_GET_CHILD_SUBREAPER round-trip with strict
##     argument validation matching Linux.
##
##     Before this batch, both options fell through to the
##     generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl, so every
##     caller (systemd in particular — it uses this during
##     service unit setup) got the wrong error.  EINVAL on an
##     option that should succeed makes systemd think we
##     don't know the option at all and switch to a fallback
##     supervision strategy (or fail outright depending on
##     the unit).
##
##     Fix: add `linux_child_subreaper: u32` to the PCB
##     (default 0).  **NOT inherited across fork** — Linux's
##     copy_signal does not propagate is_child_subreaper to
##     forked children, and a child must opt in via prctl
##     itself.  Preserved across exec (no exec hook yet —
##     same caveat as dumpable/keepcaps, but for this option
##     the exec semantics actually match: Linux preserves
##     child_subreaper across exec).
##
##     PR_SET_CHILD_SUBREAPER (36) — Linux normalises arg2 to
##     `!!arg2`, so any non-zero stores 1 and 0 clears.
##     arg3/arg4/arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.  We mirror
##     exactly and persist via the helper.
##
##     PR_GET_CHILD_SUBREAPER (37) — copies the flag into the
##     user int* at arg2.  NULL pointer is EFAULT (consistent
##     with every other PR_GET_* pointer surface).
##     arg3/arg4/arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.  Kernel
##     context (no PCB) reports the default (0).
##
##     Known limitation (deferred): orphan re-parenting to
##     the nearest subreaper ancestor is not yet wired in our
##     process lifecycle.  Setting the flag stores the bit
##     but on actual exit of a process with descendants, we
##     don't walk up the parent chain looking for the first
##     ancestor with linux_child_subreaper == 1 — we just
##     reparent to pid 1 (init).  This is invisible to most
##     userspace probes; the bit being readable back is what
##     systemd checks.  Re-parenting will need its own batch
##     once we have orphan handling at all.
##
##     Self-tests cover:
##       * PR_SET arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL (each in turn).
##       * PR_SET(0), (1), (42) all succeed (normalisation).
##       * PR_GET arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL (each in turn).
##       * PR_GET NULL pointer -> EFAULT.
##       * PR_GET kernel context -> 0 (default).
##       * pcb storage layer round-trips 0 -> 1 -> 0 -> destroy
##         -> None.
##
##     Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).


## 98. Linux ABI batch 70: real per-PCB PR_SET_THP_DISABLE /
##     PR_GET_THP_DISABLE round-trip with strict argument
##     validation matching Linux.
##
##     Before this batch, both options fell through to the
##     generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl.  Programs
##     that probe for THP control (notably JVMs, some database
##     init scripts, and systemd-oomd's tuning helpers) take
##     EINVAL as "this kernel doesn't support the option" and
##     either skip the optimisation or print scary warnings.
##
##     Fix: add `linux_thp_disable: u32` to the PCB (default 0
##     — THP enabled, system-wide policy applies).  Inherited
##     verbatim across fork (Linux copies MMF_DISABLE_THP from
##     parent mm_struct).  Preserved across exec for now —
##     Linux clears it on execve (new mm gets default flags),
##     so we'll lose strict ABI parity until we have an
##     exec-time PCB cleanup hook.  Same caveat as
##     dumpable/keepcaps; tracked in their entries too.
##
##     PR_SET_THP_DISABLE (41) — Linux normalises arg2 to
##     `!!arg2`, so any non-zero stores 1 and 0 clears.
##     arg3/arg4/arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.  We mirror
##     exactly and persist via pcb::set_thp_disable.
##
##     PR_GET_THP_DISABLE (42) — UNLIKE PR_GET_CHILD_SUBREAPER,
##     this one returns the flag *directly* as the syscall
##     return value (Linux's prctl(2) is not consistent about
##     PR_GET_* shapes — some return through the return value,
##     some write through a user pointer; THP_DISABLE is in the
##     former camp).  arg2/arg3/arg4/arg5 must all be 0 or
##     EINVAL — arg1 is *also* gated here because there is no
##     user pointer to receive the value.
##
##     Known limitation (deferred): we don't actually implement
##     transparent huge pages — every page in our design is a
##     16 KiB base page.  The flag is stored per-PCB purely for
##     ABI round-trip and has no effect on actual page
##     allocation.  If we ever add THP, the bit will already be
##     in the right place to consult.
##
##     Known limitation (deferred): exec-time reset.  Linux
##     clears MMF_DISABLE_THP on execve because the new mm
##     gets default flags.  We preserve across exec until the
##     exec hook is in place.  Same root cause as entries
##     94/95 (dumpable, keepcaps); will be fixed in the same
##     batch when the exec hook lands.
##
##     Self-tests cover:
##       * PR_SET arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL (each in turn).
##       * PR_SET(0), (1), (42) all succeed (normalisation).
##       * PR_GET arg1/arg2/arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL
##         (each in turn — arg1 included because there's no
##         user pointer here).
##       * PR_GET all-zero -> 0 (kernel context default).
##       * pcb storage layer round-trips 0 -> 1 -> 0 -> destroy
##         -> None.
##
##     Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).


## 99. Linux ABI batch 71: real per-PCB PR_SET_TIMERSLACK /
##     PR_GET_TIMERSLACK round-trip with strict argument
##     validation matching Linux.
##
##     Before this batch, both options fell through to the
##     generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl.  Android
##     userspace, systemd-oomd, power-management daemons, and
##     a handful of benchmark suites use these to tune timer
##     coalescing (smaller slack for foreground apps, larger
##     slack for background workers).  EINVAL on an option
##     that should succeed makes those callers either skip
##     the optimisation or log noise.
##
##     Fix: add two fields to the PCB:
##       * linux_timer_slack_ns (default 50_000 — Linux's
##         compile-time DEFAULT_TIMER_SLACK_NS).
##       * linux_timer_slack_default_ns (default 50_000) —
##         this is the value PR_SET_TIMERSLACK(0) restores
##         to, NOT the compile-time constant.  Linux captures
##         it at fork time from the parent's *current* slack
##         (so a child's "default" inherits whatever the
##         parent had at fork).  We mirror exactly: both
##         fields propagate verbatim across fork.  Preserved
##         across exec (Linux does NOT reset these on execve).
##
##     PR_SET_TIMERSLACK (29) — arg2 == 0 means "restore to
##     per-process default" (consult linux_timer_slack_default_ns,
##     not the compile-time constant); any non-zero stores
##     verbatim.  arg3/arg4/arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.
##     Returns 0 on success (Linux does NOT return the prior
##     value — unlike e.g. personality/umask).
##
##     PR_GET_TIMERSLACK (30) — returns the slack DIRECTLY as
##     the syscall return value (no user pointer).  Linux
##     truncates to int; we use i64 (the SyscallResult value
##     type) which covers the entire realistic range.
##     arg1..arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL (arg1 included
##     because there is no return-via-pointer mechanism).
##
##     Known limitation (deferred): our timer subsystem does
##     NOT coalesce.  The stored slack value is round-tripped
##     for ABI compatibility but has no effect on actual
##     timer expiration jitter.  When timer coalescing lands,
##     it will consult get_timer_slack_ns(pid) — the
##     plumbing is in place.
##
##     Self-tests cover:
##       * PR_SET arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL (each in turn).
##       * PR_SET(N) and PR_SET(0) both succeed in kernel
##         context (no PCB to mutate, no side effect).
##       * PR_GET arg1/arg2/arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##       * PR_GET all-zero -> 50_000 (kernel default).
##       * pcb storage round-trips default 50000 -> set
##         1_000_000 -> query 1_000_000 -> default still 50000
##         -> reset 50000 -> destroy -> None.
##
##     Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).


## 100. Linux ABI batch 72: real per-PCB PR_SET_TSC /
##      PR_GET_TSC round-trip with strict argument
##      validation matching Linux.
##
##      Before this batch, both options fell through to the
##      generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl.  Sandbox
##      runtimes (gVisor's setup probe, some seccomp-based
##      sandboxes) and benchmark harnesses use these to
##      enforce deterministic timing by trapping RDTSC.
##      EINVAL on an option that should succeed makes the
##      sandbox think we don't support the feature and either
##      fall back to a software counter or refuse to run.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_tsc_mode: u32` (default 1 =
##      PR_TSC_ENABLE) to the PCB.  Inherited verbatim across
##      fork (Linux: TIF_NOTSC is in the thread_info copy
##      path).  Preserved across exec (flush_thread does not
##      touch TIF_NOTSC).
##
##      PR_SET_TSC (26) — arg2 (our arg1) must be 1
##      (PR_TSC_ENABLE) or 2 (PR_TSC_SIGSEGV); anything else
##      including 0 is EINVAL.  arg3/arg4/arg5 must all be 0
##      or EINVAL.  Returns 0 on success.
##
##      PR_GET_TSC (25) — copies the mode into the user int*
##      at arg2 (our arg1).  NULL is EFAULT.  arg3/arg4/arg5
##      must all be 0 or EINVAL.  Kernel context (no PCB)
##      reports the default (PR_TSC_ENABLE = 1).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): we do NOT actually wire
##      the CR4.TSD bit on context switch, so the flag is
##      round-tripped purely for ABI compatibility — userspace
##      RDTSC reads never trap.  This makes the PR_GET path
##      truthful (callers see what they set) but the
##      enforcement promise of PR_SET_TSC(PR_TSC_SIGSEGV) is
##      not honoured.  Will need a context-switch hook that
##      OR's CR4.TSD into the loaded value when the next
##      task's tsc_mode == 2.  Tracked here; revisit when the
##      context-switch fast path is being touched anyway.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_SET arg1 not in {1, 2} -> EINVAL (0, 3, 99, MAX).
##        * PR_SET arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL (each in turn).
##        * PR_SET(1) and PR_SET(2) -> 0 (kernel context: no PCB).
##        * PR_GET arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL (each in turn).
##        * PR_GET NULL pointer -> EFAULT.
##        * PR_GET with scratch buffer -> writes 1 (default).
##        * pcb storage round-trips default 1 -> set 2 -> query 2 ->
##          set 1 -> query 1 -> destroy -> None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Note: first attempt of the boot test hung in the
##      [rcu] self-test after "Callback registration: OK" —
##      see entry 101 for the flake.


## 101. Boot-test flake: [rcu] self-test occasionally hangs
##      after "Callback registration: OK" line.
##
##      Observed once during batch-72 boot test (timed out at
##      the 600s mark with serial log truncating at the rcu
##      callback registration step).  Re-running immediately
##      produced a clean BOOT_OK at 23s.  No code change
##      between the two runs.
##
##      Hypothesis: the test posts an RCU callback and waits
##      for a grace period; if no quiescent state is observed
##      (e.g. the only CPU is sitting in the test loop itself
##      and never yields), the wait never completes.  Should
##      either:
##        a) explicitly advance an rcu quiescent state from
##           inside the test, or
##        b) yield to the scheduler between callback post and
##           wait, or
##        c) bound the wait with a deadline + report
##           "skipped" / "timed out" rather than hanging.
##
##      The boot path otherwise works.  Affects test
##      reliability, not production code.  Re-run the boot
##      test if it hangs here; do not block on this.  Fix
##      should land in `kernel/src/rcu/` with the test logic.


## 102. Linux ABI batch 73: real PR_GET_TID_ADDRESS round-trip.
##
##      Before this batch, option 40 fell through to the
##      generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl.  gdb's
##      `info threads` and a couple of thread-debugging
##      libraries call this on attach to find the
##      clear-child-tid futex for each thread; EINVAL makes
##      gdb fall back to a coarser detection path and emit
##      "couldn't get tid address" warnings.
##
##      Fix: wire option 40 to `lookup_clear_child_tid(task)`
##      (already exists in thread_clone.rs — was previously
##      gated `#[cfg(test)]`, now always available with a
##      doc comment explaining the user).  Returns 0 when no
##      address has been registered (i.e., no prior
##      `set_tid_address` and no `CLONE_CHILD_CLEARTID`),
##      which matches Linux's behaviour of returning the
##      zero-initialised slot.
##
##      arg3/arg4/arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.  arg2 (our
##      arg1) is the user `void**` to write the 8-byte
