##      address into; NULL is EFAULT.
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): Linux gates this option
##      on CAP_SYS_ADMIN-or-PTRACE permission for non-self
##      queries; gdb attaches with PTRACE so the check is
##      trivially satisfied in practice.  We do not enforce
##      capabilities at all yet; revisit when the capability
##      framework lands.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL (each in turn).
##        * NULL pointer -> EFAULT.
##        * Scratch buffer pre-filled with sentinel; after
##          dispatch the buffer reads 0 (kernel context: no
##          set_tid_address registration).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 25s).


## 103. Linux ABI batch 74: real per-PCB PR_MCE_KILL /
##      PR_MCE_KILL_GET round-trip with strict argument
##      validation matching Linux.
##
##      Before this batch, both options fell through to the
##      generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl.
##      Container runtimes (Podman, container-init) and
##      OOM-handling daemons set this to PR_MCE_KILL_EARLY on
##      processes that hold large shared-memory regions where
##      recovery isn't worth the latency.  EINVAL on an
##      option that should succeed silently disables the
##      optimisation.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_mce_kill_policy: u32` (default 2 =
##      PR_MCE_KILL_DEFAULT) to the PCB.  Encode the
##      user-visible value directly (0=LATE, 1=EARLY,
##      2=DEFAULT) rather than mirroring Linux's two-bit
##      PF_MCE_PROCESS / PF_MCE_EARLY encoding — makes the
##      GET path a trivial read.
##
##      Inherited verbatim across fork (Linux: both PF_MCE
##      bits are in task_struct::flags copy path).  Preserved
##      across exec (flush_thread does not touch them).
##
##      PR_MCE_KILL (33) — strict Linux semantics:
##        * arg2 (our arg1) = PR_MCE_KILL_CLEAR (0) or
##          PR_MCE_KILL_SET (1); other values -> EINVAL.
##        * arg3 (our arg2) ignored on CLEAR (must be 0 or
##          EINVAL); on SET must be PR_MCE_KILL_LATE (0),
##          PR_MCE_KILL_EARLY (1), or PR_MCE_KILL_DEFAULT (2);
##          others -> EINVAL.
##        * arg4/arg5 must be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * CLEAR and SET-with-DEFAULT both store DEFAULT
##          (matches Linux clearing both PF bits).
##        * Returns 0 on success.
##
##      PR_MCE_KILL_GET (34) — returns the policy DIRECTLY as
##      the syscall return value (no user pointer).
##      arg1..arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.  Kernel context
##      reports the default (2).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): we do not implement
##      machine-check exception handling at all.  The stored
##      policy is round-tripped for ABI compatibility only and
##      has no effect on what actually happens on MCE.  When
##      MCE handling lands it should consult
##      get_mce_kill_policy(pid) to decide between
##      SIGBUS-now vs. let-recovery-try-first.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_MCE_KILL arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 not in {0, 1} -> EINVAL.
##        * CLEAR + arg2 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * SET + arg2 not in {0, 1, 2} -> EINVAL.
##        * CLEAR / SET(0) / SET(1) / SET(2) all -> 0 in
##          kernel context.
##        * PR_MCE_KILL_GET arg1..arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context -> 2 (default).
##        * pcb storage layer round-trips
##          DEFAULT -> EARLY -> LATE -> DEFAULT -> destroy -> None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).


## 104. Linux ABI batch 75: PR_TASK_PERF_EVENTS_DISABLE /
##      PR_TASK_PERF_EVENTS_ENABLE — bare success.
##
##      Before this batch, options 31 and 32 fell through to
##      EINVAL in sys_prctl.  Perf-aware userspace (`perf stat
##      -p $$`, valgrind's CALLGRIND_START/STOP_INSTRUMENTATION
##      wrappers, glibc's __libc_pre_main hot-restart hooks)
##      calls these to bracket measurement regions.  EINVAL
##      makes them emit "could not disable perf events,
##      continuing" warnings on every run.
##
##      Fix: return 0 from both.  We do not implement a perf
##      event subsystem, so there is nothing attached to
##      disable / enable.  No per-PCB state is needed — there
##      is no corresponding PR_GET for these options on Linux,
##      so the bit is purely a side-effect command.  If we
##      ever add perf events, the disable/enable handlers
##      will iterate the per-task event list at that time;
##      until then, success is the correct user-visible
##      answer.
##
##      Linux ignores arg2..arg5 for both options; we mirror
##      exactly (no validation), so probe code that passes
##      garbage in the extra args still sees success.
##
##      Self-tests cover both options with garbage in
##      arg1..arg5 — must return 0.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).


## 105. Linux ABI batch 76: real per-PCB PR_SET_MDWE /
##      PR_GET_MDWE round-trip (Memory-Deny-Write-Execute),
##      with sticky-monotone semantics matching Linux.
##
##      Before this batch, both options fell through to the
##      generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl.  Modern
##      sandbox / hardening layers (systemd's
##      MemoryDenyWriteExecute=, chromium's renderer
##      sandbox, openssh's seccomp-MDWE combo, podman's
##      hardened-container preset) call PR_SET_MDWE during
##      init.  EINVAL there silently disables MDWE and the
##      process falls back to weaker W^X enforcement, which
##      is a measurable hardening regression.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_mdwe_bits: u32` (default 0) to the
##      PCB with strict sticky-monotone semantics matching
##      Linux's task_struct::mm->flags MDWE bit pair:
##        * Bit 0 = PR_MDWE_REFUSE_EXEC_GAIN (1)
##        * Bit 1 = PR_MDWE_NO_INHERIT       (2)
##        * Valid mask = 3.
##
##      Inheritance: verbatim across fork.  Linux duplicates
##      `mm->flags` MDWE bits via `dup_mm_flags`, so the child
##      starts with the same MDWE policy as the parent
##      regardless of NO_INHERIT (which is an exec-time, not
##      fork-time, gate).  fork_create copies the field
##      verbatim.
##
##      Across exec: Linux clears the bits iff
##      PR_MDWE_NO_INHERIT was set.  We preserve
##      unconditionally for now (same caveat as
##      DUMPABLE/KEEPCAPS — exec hook not yet landed).  When
##      the exec hook lands it must check NO_INHERIT and
##      clear linux_mdwe_bits on the new image.
##
##      PR_SET_MDWE (65) — strict Linux semantics:
##        * arg3 / arg4 / arg5 (our arg2/arg3/arg4) must all
##          be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * arg2 (our arg1) > u32::MAX -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 with bits outside the valid mask -> EINVAL.
##        * NO_INHERIT without REFUSE_EXEC_GAIN -> EINVAL
##          (Linux: NO_INHERIT is meaningless without the
##          refusal bit).
##        * Sticky monotone: once set non-zero, the only
##          legal subsequent value is the same value;
##          attempting to clear or change -> EPERM.  Matches
##          Linux's prctl_set_mdwe which refuses any
##          transition out of a previously-set state.
##        * Returns 0 on success.
##
##      PR_GET_MDWE (66) — returns the bits DIRECTLY as the
##      syscall return value (no user pointer).  arg1..arg5
##      must all be 0 or EINVAL.  Kernel context reports 0.
##
##      Known limitation (deferred — IMPORTANT): mmap and
##      mprotect do NOT yet consult linux_mdwe_bits.  This
##      means PR_SET_MDWE succeeds, PR_GET_MDWE truthfully
##      reports the value, but the security promise of
##      refusing PROT_WRITE | PROT_EXEC (or transitioning a
##      mapping to PROT_EXEC after having been writable) is
##      NOT honoured.  A process that sets MDWE today still
##      gets W+X mappings.  This is an active hardening
##      regression vs Linux for any caller that trusts the
##      kernel to enforce MDWE after a successful PR_SET.
##      Fix: when mmap / mprotect land, both must consult
##      get_mdwe_bits(pid) and, if REFUSE_EXEC_GAIN is set,
##      reject any mapping that grants PROT_EXEC while
##      having had PROT_WRITE at any point, returning
##      EACCES.  Track this in the mmap / mprotect work
##      items.
##
##      Storage layer (get_mdwe_bits / set_mdwe_bits) does
##      NOT enforce monotonicity.  The sticky-monotone EPERM
##      is enforced at the syscall surface only, so future
##      kernel callers (e.g. exec hardening reloads) can
##      adjust the bits without violating user-visible
##      stickiness.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_SET_MDWE arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 > u32::MAX -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 with bit outside valid mask (e.g. 4) ->
##          EINVAL.
##        * NO_INHERIT (2) without REFUSE_EXEC_GAIN ->
##          EINVAL.
##        * Valid values 0, 1, 3 all return 0 in kernel
##          context (kernel-context path skips state mutation
##          so stickiness check doesn't reject the sequence).
##        * PR_GET_MDWE arg1..arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context PR_GET_MDWE -> 0 (default).
##        * pcb storage round-trip:
##          0 -> 1 -> 3 -> 0 (helper accepts because surface
##          enforces monotonicity, not the helper) ->
##          destroy -> None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).


## 106. Linux ABI batch 77: PR_GET_TIMING / PR_SET_TIMING —
##      Linux-faithful constant-mode handling.
##
##      Before this batch options 13 and 14 fell through to
##      the generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  Boot-time
##      profilers and accounting wrappers (sysstat's `sar`,
##      gnu coreutils `time` when probing for high-resolution
##      timing, and a handful of older glibc init paths)
##      issue PR_GET_TIMING at startup to detect whether the
##      kernel offers timestamp-based timing.  EINVAL there
##      makes them emit a "kernel does not support timing
##      mode selection" warning on every run.
##
##      Linux's prctl_set_timing accepts only
##      PR_TIMING_STATISTICAL (0); PR_TIMING_TIMESTAMP (1)
##      was reserved in the original API but never
##      implemented and is rejected with EINVAL.  arg3..arg5
##      are ignored entirely.  prctl_get_timing always
##      returns PR_TIMING_STATISTICAL (0) and ignores
##      arg2..arg5.  Mirror exactly.
##
##      No per-PCB storage is needed because there is only
##      one valid mode — PR_GET is a constant.
##
##      PR_SET_TIMING (14):
##        * arg1 == 0 -> 0 (success).
##        * arg1 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg2..arg5 not validated.
##
##      PR_GET_TIMING (13):
##        * Always returns 0.
##        * arg1..arg5 not validated.
##
##      No known limitations: this option is purely a probe
##      surface in modern userspace because Linux itself
##      never implemented timestamp-mode timing.  Anyone
##      relying on it for real measurement uses
##      CLOCK_PROCESS_CPUTIME_ID or `getrusage`.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_SET_TIMING(0) -> 0.
##        * PR_SET_TIMING(1) -> EINVAL.
##        * PR_SET_TIMING(0xdead) -> EINVAL.
##        * PR_SET_TIMING(0) with garbage arg2..arg5 -> 0.
##        * PR_GET_TIMING with garbage arg1..arg5 -> 0.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).


## 107. Linux ABI batch 78: PR_GET_SECCOMP — truthful "no
##      seccomp installed" report.
##
##      Before this batch option 21 fell through to the
##      generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  Every sandbox
##      library (libseccomp, audit-libs, container runtimes,
##      python's pyseccomp startup check, golang's seccomp
##      bindings) probes PR_GET_SECCOMP at startup to learn
##      whether a filter is already installed (e.g. by a
##      parent process) before deciding what to install.
##      EINVAL there looks like a kernel bug because Linux
##      `prctl_get_seccomp` cannot fail.
##
##      Linux semantics: returns `current->seccomp.mode`
##      (0=DISABLED, 1=STRICT, 2=FILTER) with no argument
##      validation.  arg1..arg5 are ignored entirely.  Cannot
##      fail.  We mirror exactly and always return 0
##      (DISABLED) because we have no seccomp infrastructure.
##
##      No per-PCB storage: until we actually implement
##      seccomp, the answer is a constant.
##
##      Companion PR_SET_SECCOMP (22) is INTENTIONALLY left at
##      the EINVAL catch-all.  Pretending to install a seccomp
##      filter would be an active security lie: the sandbox
##      would believe its syscall whitelist was enforced when
##      it isn't.  The correct answer for PR_SET_SECCOMP is
##      ENOSYS ("kernel does not implement this feature") so
##      sandboxes can fall back to weaker hardening rather
##      than crash on EINVAL, but changing the errno is a
##      behavioural change with its own ABI surface area
##      (sandbox-init code paths that distinguish EINVAL vs
##      ENOSYS) — defer to a future batch where the change can
##      be paired with audits of the major sandbox callers we
##      care about (chromium renderer, firefox content
##      process, systemd-nspawn).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): PR_SET_SECCOMP still
##      returns EINVAL; should be ENOSYS once we've decided
##      the migration story for the few callers that probe
##      via PR_SET with a NULL filter to feature-detect.  We
##      should NEVER return success from PR_SET_SECCOMP until
##      we have a real BPF interpreter — that would be a
##      security regression.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_GET_SECCOMP with zero arg1..arg5 -> 0.
##        * PR_GET_SECCOMP with garbage arg1..arg5 -> 0
##          (mirroring Linux's ignore-extra-args).
##        * PR_SET_SECCOMP(FILTER, NULL, 0, 0, 0) STILL ->
##          EINVAL (anti-regression: ensure we haven't
##          accidentally started lying about seccomp support).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).


## 108. Linux ABI batch 79: per-PCB PR_SET_IO_FLUSHER /
##      PR_GET_IO_FLUSHER round-trip with strict argument
##      validation matching Linux.
##
##      Before this batch options 57 and 58 fell through to
##      the generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  Storage
##      daemons (drbd-worker, multipathd, nbd-client,
##      dm_crypt_write) call PR_SET_IO_FLUSHER on themselves
##      at init.  Linux uses the PR_IO_FLUSHER bit in
##      task->flags to grant `__GFP_MEMALLOC`-style fast-path
##      access in the memory-reclaim path — without this
##      mark, a flusher can self-deadlock by needing free
##      pages to make writeback progress while reclaim is
##      waiting for writeback to finish.  EINVAL there made
##      every storage daemon log a startup warning.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_io_flusher: u32` (default 0, only
##      values 0 and 1 are legal) to the PCB.  Inherited
##      verbatim across fork (Linux: `PR_IO_FLUSHER` is a
##      `task->flags` bit copied by `copy_process`).
##      Preserved across exec (flush_thread does not touch
##      it).
##
##      PR_SET_IO_FLUSHER (57) — strict Linux semantics:
##        * arg3 / arg4 / arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * arg2 (our arg1) must be 0 or 1; anything else
##          (including out-of-range u64 values like
##          u64::MAX) returns EINVAL.
##        * Returns 0 on success.
##
##      Linux also requires CAP_SYS_RESOURCE; we don't
##      enforce capabilities at all yet, so any caller can
##      set the flag.  When the capability framework lands,
##      this surface should consult it.  Tracked as a known
##      limitation here.
##
##      PR_GET_IO_FLUSHER (58) — returns the flag value (0
##      or 1) DIRECTLY as the syscall return (no user
##      pointer).  Linux requires CAP_SYS_RESOURCE; we don't
##      enforce.  arg1..arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.
##      Kernel context reports 0 (the default).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): we have no memory
##      reclaim or writeback path yet, so the flag is
##      round-tripped for ABI compatibility only and has no
##      effect on what actually happens under memory
##      pressure.  When reclaim lands it must consult
##      get_io_flusher(pid) and grant the GFP_MEMALLOC fast
##      path for tasks with the flag set, else flushers will
##      deadlock under pressure just like a Linux build with
##      this feature disabled.
##
##      Storage layer (get_io_flusher / set_io_flusher) does
##      not validate values — the helper accepts arbitrary
##      u32s so test fixtures can manipulate freely.  The
##      syscall surface is the validation site.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_SET_IO_FLUSHER arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 not in {0, 1} -> EINVAL (2, 3, 0xff,
##          u64::MAX).
##        * (0) / (1) -> 0 in kernel context.
##        * PR_GET_IO_FLUSHER arg1..arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context PR_GET_IO_FLUSHER -> 0.
##        * pcb storage round-trip:
##          0 -> 1 -> 0 -> destroy -> None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s) on
##      retry after one flake at RCU 'Callback invoked: OK'
##      boundary (the same RCU flake region documented in
##      todo 101).


## 109. Linux ABI batch 80: PR_GET_SPECULATION_CTRL /
##      PR_SET_SPECULATION_CTRL — consistent "not affected"
##      stance.
##
##      Before this batch options 52 and 53 fell through to
##      the generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  Container
##      runtimes (podman, cri-o), browsers (Chromium
##      renderer sandbox), and OpenSSL since CVE-2018-3639
##      (Spectre v4) probe PR_GET_SPECULATION_CTRL at init
##      to learn whether the per-task SSBD / IBRS / L1D
##      mitigations are installed.  EINVAL there looks like
##      a kernel bug and forces them to install the
##      mitigation themselves, which on a non-affected CPU
##      is a measurable performance regression for nothing.
##
##      Linux semantics:
##        * PR_GET_SPECULATION_CTRL (52):
##          - arg2 (our arg1) = which:
##              PR_SPEC_STORE_BYPASS = 0
##              PR_SPEC_INDIRECT_BRANCH = 1
##              PR_SPEC_L1D_FLUSH = 2
##            Unknown -> ENODEV.
##          - arg3/arg4/arg5 must be 0 or EINVAL.
##          - Returns a bitfield:
##              PR_SPEC_NOT_AFFECTED = 0
##              PR_SPEC_PRCTL = 1
##              PR_SPEC_ENABLE = 2
##              PR_SPEC_DISABLE = 4
##              PR_SPEC_FORCE_DISABLE = 8
##              PR_SPEC_DISABLE_NOEXEC = 16
##            On a CPU that doesn't need the mitigation
##            (Apple Silicon, modern Intel post-mitigations,
##            most AMD), Linux returns NOT_AFFECTED (0).
##
##        * PR_SET_SPECULATION_CTRL (53):
##          - arg2 (our arg1) = which (same constants).
##          - arg3 (our arg2) = ctrl (ENABLE/DISABLE/…).
##          - arg4/arg5 must be 0 or EINVAL.
##          - Unknown which -> ENODEV.
##          - Known which on a NOT_AFFECTED CPU -> ENXIO
##            ("the mitigation isn't controllable because
##            the CPU isn't vulnerable").
##
##      Our stance: PR_SPEC_NOT_AFFECTED for all three
##      supported features.  ENODEV for unknown features.
##      ENXIO from PR_SET for known features (matching the
##      PR_GET NOT_AFFECTED report — internally consistent).
##
##      Known limitation (IMPORTANT, deferred): we report
##      NOT_AFFECTED even on CPUs that ARE affected (most
##      x86_64).  This is defensible for a hypervisor-only
##      kernel running in QEMU where the host already
##      mitigates, but if we ever boot on bare metal and
##      expose raw indirect branches we MUST either:
##        a. Implement IBRS/SSBD/L1D-flush ourselves and
##           report PR_SPEC_PRCTL | PR_SPEC_ENABLE (or
##           whatever the current state is), or
##        b. Restrict bare-metal boot to AMD CPUs known not
##           to need IBRS.
##      Otherwise sandbox callers will trust our "not
##      affected" report and skip the mitigation they
##      should have installed.  Track when we add bare-metal
##      boot support.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_GET valid which (0, 1, 2) -> 0.
##        * PR_GET arg2..arg4 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * PR_GET unknown which (3, 0xff, u64::MAX) ->
##          ENODEV.
##        * PR_SET known which (0/1/2) with any ctrl
##          (ENABLE/DISABLE/FORCE_DISABLE) -> ENXIO.
##        * PR_SET arg3/arg4 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * PR_SET unknown which -> ENODEV.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).


## 110. Linux ABI batch 81: per-PCB PR_SET_MEMORY_MERGE /
##      PR_GET_MEMORY_MERGE round-trip (KSM opt-in).
##
##      Before this batch options 67 and 68 fell through to
##      the generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  VM hosts
##      (qemu/kvm setup paths), JVM-per-container deployments,
##      and Python multi-process pools all probe these
##      options at startup to opt into Kernel Same-page
##      Merging.  EINVAL there forces the runtime to either
##      assume KSM is unsupported (skipping a measurable
##      memory-savings win for VM workloads) or emit a
##      warning on every spawn.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_memory_merge: u32` (default 0, only
##      values 0 and 1 are legal) to the PCB.  Inherited
##      verbatim across fork (Linux: MMF_VM_MERGE_ANY is in
##      `mm->flags` and survives `dup_mmap`).  Preserved
##      across exec (the bit is in the mm-flags subset that
##      survives flush_old_exec).
##
##      PR_SET_MEMORY_MERGE (67):
##        * arg3/arg4/arg5 must be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * arg2 (our arg1) must be 0 or 1; else EINVAL.
##        * Linux additionally requires NO_NEW_PRIVS or
##          CAP_SYS_RESOURCE; we don't enforce caps.
##        * Returns 0 on success.
##
##      PR_GET_MEMORY_MERGE (68):
##        * arg1..arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * Returns the flag DIRECTLY as the syscall return.
##        * Kernel context reports 0 (the default).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): we do not implement KSM
##      at all.  The flag round-trips for ABI compatibility
##      but no actual page merging happens.  When KSM lands,
##      the VMA-walk on each mmap must consult
##      `get_memory_merge(pid)` and queue mergeable anonymous
##      regions onto the KSM scanner's worklist.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL on PR_SET.
##        * arg1 not in {0, 1} -> EINVAL (2, 3, 0xff,
##          u64::MAX).
##        * (0) / (1) -> 0.
##        * arg1..arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL on PR_GET.
##        * Kernel context PR_GET -> 0.
##        * pcb storage round-trip 0 -> 1 -> 0 -> destroy ->
##          None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).


## 111. Linux ABI batch 82: per-PCB PR_CAP_AMBIENT
##      round-trip with strict argument validation matching
##      Linux.
##
##      Before this batch option 47 fell through to the
##      generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  systemd
##      (every service unit with `AmbientCapabilities=`),
##      container runtimes (podman, docker, runc), and
##      `capsh --addamb` userspace tooling probe this
##      option to install ambient capabilities — the only
##      capability set that a non-setuid, non-file-cap
##      execve preserves across the binary boundary.  EINVAL
##      there makes systemd refuse to start any unit that
##      relies on ambient caps (e.g. nm-online with
##      CAP_NET_ADMIN).
##
##      Fix: add `linux_ambient_caps: u64` (default 0 = empty
##      set) to the PCB, indexed bitwise by capability number.
##      Inherited verbatim across fork (Linux: `cred->ambient`
##      is copied by `prepare_cred`).  Preserved across exec
##      — this is the defining property of the ambient set
##      vs the inheritable set.
##
##      Added `LINUX_CAP_LAST_CAP = 40`
##      (`CAP_CHECKPOINT_RESTORE`, added in Linux 5.9) as a
##      public constant.  Any cap number above 40 is EINVAL
##      at the syscall surface.  The storage helper accepts
##      arbitrary u64 masks so test fixtures can probe
##      boundary cases.
##
##      Five storage helpers:
##        get_ambient_caps(pid) -> Option<u64>
##        set_ambient_caps(pid, val) -> Option<u64>
##        raise_ambient_cap(pid, cap) -> Option<u32>
##        lower_ambient_cap(pid, cap) -> Option<u32>
##        is_ambient_cap_set(pid, cap) -> Option<u32>
##
##      PR_CAP_AMBIENT (47) — Linux semantics, sub-op via
##      arg2 (our arg1):
##        * PR_CAP_AMBIENT_IS_SET (1):
##          - arg3/arg4 (our arg3/arg4) must be 0 -> EINVAL.
##          - arg2 (our arg2) = cap; > CAP_LAST_CAP -> EINVAL.
##          - Returns 0 or 1 directly.
##        * PR_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE (2):
##          - Same arg validation.
##          - Linux additionally checks the cap is in both
##            permitted AND inheritable, and that
##            SECURE_NO_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE is unset.  We
##            don't enforce capabilities anywhere, so we
##            skip both checks and grant — known limitation,
##            tracked here.
##          - Returns 0.
##        * PR_CAP_AMBIENT_LOWER (3):
##          - Same arg validation.
##          - Returns 0.
##        * PR_CAP_AMBIENT_CLEAR_ALL (4):
##          - arg2/arg3/arg4 must all be 0 -> EINVAL.
##          - Returns 0.
##        * Any other op (including 0) -> EINVAL.
##
##      Known limitations (deferred):
##        1. We do not enforce capabilities anywhere; every
##           process effectively has all caps in permitted
##           and effective.  PR_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE therefore
##           grants without checking permitted /
##           inheritable — when the capability framework
##           lands, this surface MUST verify the cap is in
##           both sets and return EPERM otherwise.
##        2. We do not model securebits, so we do not
##           enforce SECURE_NO_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE.  When
##           securebits land (likely paired with PR_GET/SET
##           SECUREBITS), this check must gate RAISE too.
##        3. The stored ambient mask has no effect on
##           anything yet — it's pure round-trip.  When
##           capability enforcement lands, every privilege-
##           bearing syscall (mount, kexec_load, reboot, …)
##           must consult both the ambient and effective
##           sets to decide whether to permit the call.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * op == 0 or > 4 -> EINVAL (incl. u64::MAX).
##        * RAISE/LOWER/IS_SET with cap > 40 (41, 0xff,
##          u64::MAX) -> EINVAL.
##        * RAISE/LOWER/IS_SET with arg3 / arg4 != 0 ->
##          EINVAL.
##        * CLEAR_ALL with arg2 / arg3 / arg4 != 0 ->
##          EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context: every op returns 0 (IS_SET
##          unwrap_or(0) path).
##        * pcb storage round-trip:
##          - get returns Some(0) initially.
##          - raise CAP_NET_ADMIN twice: was-set
##            transitions 0 -> 1; second raise reports 1.
##          - get returns the union of raised bits.
##          - lower returns was-set; double-lower returns 0.
##          - set_ambient_caps accepts arbitrary u64 bits
##            (helper bypasses cap-validity check).
##          - destroy -> all helpers return None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
